 <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
  <div class="mw-parser-output">
   <p>In <a href="/wiki/Probability_theory" title="Probability theory">probability theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a>, the <b>cumulants</b> <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">κ<sub>n</sub></span> of a <a href="/wiki/Probability_distribution" title="Probability distribution">probability distribution</a> are a set of quantities that provide an alternative to the <a href="/wiki/Moment_(mathematics)" title="Moment (mathematics)">moments</a> of the distribution. The moments determine the cumulants in the sense that any two probability distributions whose moments are identical will have identical cumulants as well, and similarly the cumulants determine the moments. </p>
   <p>The first cumulant is the <a href="/wiki/Mean" title="Mean">mean</a>, the second cumulant is the <a href="/wiki/Variance" title="Variance">variance</a>, and the third cumulant is the same as the third <a href="/wiki/Central_moment" title="Central moment">central moment</a>. But fourth and higher-order cumulants are not equal to central moments. In some cases theoretical treatments of problems in terms of cumulants are simpler than those using moments. In particular, when two or more random variables are <a href="/wiki/Statistically_independent" class="mw-redirect" title="Statistically independent">statistically independent</a>, the <i>n</i><sup>th</sup>-order cumulant of their sum is equal to the sum of their <i>n</i><sup>th</sup>-order cumulants. As well, the third and higher-order cumulants of a <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">normal distribution</a> are zero, and it is the only distribution with this property. </p>
   <p>Just as for moments, where <i>joint moments</i> are used for collections of random variables, it is possible to define <i>joint cumulants</i>. </p>

   <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
   <p>The cumulants of a random variable <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">X</span> are defined using the <b>cumulant-generating function</b> <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i>(<i>t</i>)</span>, which is the <a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">natural logarithm</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Moment-generating_function" title="Moment-generating function">moment-generating function</a>: </p>

   <p>If the moment-generating function does not exist, the cumulants can be defined in terms of the relationship between cumulants and moments discussed later. </p> 
   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_definition_of_the_cumulant_generating_function">Alternative definition of the cumulant generating function</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Alternative definition of the cumulant generating function">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <p>Some writers<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> prefer to define the cumulant-generating function as the natural logarithm of the <a href="/wiki/Characteristic_function_(probability_theory)" title="Characteristic function (probability theory)">characteristic function</a>, which is sometimes also called the <b><i>second</i> characteristic function,</b><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
       <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle H(t)=\log \operatorname {E} \left[e^{itX}\right]=\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }\kappa _{n}{\frac {(it)^{n}}{n!}}=\mu it-\sigma ^{2}{\frac {t^{2}}{2}}+\cdots }">

        </semantics> 
       </math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1d1e3d1e389cd3ae8376b9c31b0368c9c4519646" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:53.134ex; height:6.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle H(t)=\log \operatorname {E} \left[e^{itX}\right]=\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }\kappa _{n}{\frac {(it)^{n}}{n!}}=\mu it-\sigma ^{2}{\frac {t^{2}}{2}}+\cdots }"></span>
    </dd>
   </dl> 
   <p>An advantage of <span class="texhtml"><i>H</i>(<i>t</i>)</span>—in some sense the function <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i>(<i>t</i>)</span> evaluated for purely imaginary arguments—is that <span class="texhtml">E(<i>e</i><sup><i>itX</i></sup>)</span> is well defined for all real values of <span class="texhtml"><i>t</i></span> even when <span class="texhtml">E(<i>e</i><sup><i>tX</i></sup>)</span> is not well defined for all real values of <span class="texhtml"><i>t</i></span>, such as can occur when there is "too much" probability that <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i></span> has a large magnitude. Although the function <span class="texhtml"><i>H</i>(<i>t</i>)</span> will be well defined, it will nonetheless mimic <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i>(<i>t</i>)</span> in terms of the length of its <a href="/wiki/Maclaurin_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Maclaurin series">Maclaurin series</a>, which may not extend beyond (or, rarely, even to) linear order in the argument&nbsp;<span class="texhtml"><i>t</i></span>, and in particular the number of cumulants that are well defined will not change. Nevertheless, even when <span class="texhtml"><i>H</i>(<i>t</i>)</span> does not have a long Maclaurin series, it can be used directly in analyzing and, particularly, adding random variables. Both the <a href="/wiki/Cauchy_distribution" title="Cauchy distribution">Cauchy distribution</a> (also called the Lorentzian) and more generally, <a href="/wiki/Stable_distribution" title="Stable distribution">stable distributions</a> (related to the Lévy distribution) are examples of distributions for which the power-series expansions of the generating functions have only finitely many well-defined terms. </p> 
   <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses_in_statistics">Uses in statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Uses in statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
   <p>Working with cumulants can have an advantage over using moments because for statistically independent random variables <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>Y</i></span>, </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

         <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
          {\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}K_{X+Y}(t)&amp;=\log \operatorname {E} \left[e^{t(X+Y)}\right]\\[5pt]&amp;=\log \left(\operatorname {E} \left[e^{tX}\right]\operatorname {E} \left[e^{tY}\right]\right)\\[5pt]&amp;=\log \operatorname {E} \left[e^{tX}\right]+\log \operatorname {E} \left[e^{tY}\right]\\[5pt]&amp;=K_{X}(t)+K_{Y}(t),\end{aligned}}}
         </annotation> 
        </semantics> 
       </math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/839b2e386274beb3ba4151da6e6e16d034c4ec73" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -8.415ex; margin-bottom: -0.256ex; width:35.712ex; height:18.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}K_{X+Y}(t)&amp;=\log \operatorname {E} \left[e^{t(X+Y)}\right]\\[5pt]&amp;=\log \left(\operatorname {E} \left[e^{tX}\right]\operatorname {E} \left[e^{tY}\right]\right)\\[5pt]&amp;=\log \operatorname {E} \left[e^{tX}\right]+\log \operatorname {E} \left[e^{tY}\right]\\[5pt]&amp;=K_{X}(t)+K_{Y}(t),\end{aligned}}}"></span>
    </dd>
   </dl> 
   <p>so that each cumulant of a sum of independent random variables is the sum of the corresponding cumulants of the <a href="/wiki/Addend" class="mw-redirect" title="Addend">addends</a>. That is, when the addends are statistically independent, the mean of the sum is the sum of the means, the variance of the sum is the sum of the variances, the third cumulant (which happens to be the third central moment) of the sum is the sum of the third cumulants, and so on for each order of cumulant. </p>
   <p>A distribution with given cumulants <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">κ<sub>n</sub></span> can be approximated through an <a href="/wiki/Edgeworth_series" title="Edgeworth series">Edgeworth series</a>. </p> 
   <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulants_of_some_discrete_probability_distributions">Cumulants of some discrete probability distributions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Cumulants of some discrete probability distributions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
   <ul>
    <li>The constant random variables <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i> = <i>μ</i></span>. The cumulant generating function is <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i>(<i>t</i>) =<i>μt</i></span>. The first cumulant is <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub>1</sub> = <i>K</i>&nbsp;'(0) = <i>μ</i></span> and the other cumulants are zero, <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub>2</sub> = <i>κ</i><sub>3</sub> = <i>κ</i><sub>4</sub> = ... = 0</span>.</li> 
    <li>The <a href="/wiki/Bernoulli_distribution" title="Bernoulli distribution">Bernoulli distributions</a>, (number of successes in one trial with probability <span class="texhtml"><i>p</i></span> of success). The cumulant generating function is <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i>(<i>t</i>) = log(1&nbsp;−&nbsp;<i>p</i>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>p</i>e<sup><i>t</i></sup>)</span>. The first cumulants are <span class="texhtml">κ<sub>1</sub> = <i>K</i>&nbsp;'(0) = <i>p</i></span> and <span class="texhtml">κ<sub>2</sub> = <i>K′′</i>(0) = <i>p</i>·(1&nbsp;−&nbsp;<i>p</i>)</span>. The cumulants satisfy a recursion formula</li>
   </ul> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \kappa _{n+1}=p(1-p){\frac {d\kappa _{n}}{dp}}.}"> 
          <semantics> 
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0"> 
             <msub> 
              <mi>
               κ
               <!-- κ -->
              </mi> 
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
               <mi>
                n
               </mi> 
               <mo>
                +
               </mo> 
               <mn>
                1
               </mn> 
              </mrow> 
             </msub> 
             <mo>
              =
             </mo> 
             <mi>
              p
             </mi> 
             <mo stretchy="false">
              (
             </mo> 
             <mn>
              1
             </mn> 
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo> 
             <mi>
              p
             </mi> 
             <mo stretchy="false">
              )
             </mo> 
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
              <mfrac> 
               <mrow> 
                <mi>
                 d
                </mi> 
                <msub> 
                 <mi>
                  κ
                  <!-- κ -->
                 </mi> 
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
                  <mi>
                   n
                  </mi> 
                 </mrow> 
                </msub> 
               </mrow> 
               <mrow> 
                <mi>
                 d
                </mi> 
                <mi>
                 p
                </mi> 
               </mrow> 
              </mfrac> 
             </mrow> 
             <mo>
              .
             </mo> 
            </mstyle> 
           </mrow> 
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \kappa _{n+1}=p(1-p){\frac {d\kappa _{n}}{dp}}.}
           </annotation> 
          </semantics> 
         </math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bdd91d4585237e39fbb45744c955337a40faaee1" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:21.164ex; height:5.843ex;" alt="\kappa _{n+1}=p(1-p){\frac {d\kappa _{n}}{dp}}."></span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
    </dd>
   </dl> 
   <ul>
    <li>The <a href="/wiki/Geometric_distribution" title="Geometric distribution">geometric distributions</a>, (number of failures before one success with probability <span class="texhtml"><i>p</i></span> of success on each trial). The cumulant generating function is <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i>(<i>t</i>) = log(<i>p</i> / (1 + (<i>p</i>&nbsp;−&nbsp;1)e<sup><i>t</i></sup>))</span>. The first cumulants are <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub>1</sub> = <i>K′</i>(0) = <i>p</i><sup>−1</sup> − 1</span>, and <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub>2</sub> = <i>K′′</i>(0) = <i>κ</i><sub>1</sub><i>p</i><sup>−1</sup></span>. Substituting <span class="texhtml"><i>p</i> = (<i>μ</i> + 1)<sup>−1</sup></span> gives <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i>(<i>t</i>) = −log(1 + <i>μ</i>(1−e<sup><i>t</i></sup>))</span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub>1</sub> = <i>μ</i></span>.</li> 
    <li>The <a href="/wiki/Poisson_distribution" title="Poisson distribution">Poisson distributions</a>. The cumulant generating function is <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i>(<i>t</i>) = <i>μ</i>(e<sup><i>t</i></sup>&nbsp;−&nbsp;1)</span>. All cumulants are equal to the parameter: <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub>1</sub> = <i>κ</i><sub>2</sub> = <i>κ</i><sub>3</sub> = ... = <i>μ</i></span>.</li> 
    <li>The <a href="/wiki/Binomial_distribution" title="Binomial distribution">binomial distributions</a>, (number of successes in <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> <a href="/wiki/Statistical_independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Statistical independence">independent</a> trials with probability <span class="texhtml"><i>p</i></span> of success on each trial). The special case <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i> = 1</span> is a Bernoulli distribution. Every cumulant is just <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> times the corresponding cumulant of the corresponding Bernoulli distribution. The cumulant generating function is <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i>(<i>t</i>) = <i>n</i>&nbsp;log(1&nbsp;−&nbsp;<i>p</i>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>p</i>e<sup><i>t</i></sup>)</span>. The first cumulants are <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub>1</sub> = <i>K′</i>(0) = <i>np</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub>2</sub> = <i>K′′</i>(0) = <i>κ</i><sub>1</sub>(1 − <i>p</i>)</span>. Substituting <span class="texhtml"><i>p</i> = μ·<i>n</i><sup>−1</sup></span> gives <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i>&nbsp;'(<i>t</i>) = ((μ<sup>−1</sup> − <i>n</i><sup>−1</sup>)·e<sup>−<i>t</i></sup> + <i>n</i><sup>−1</sup>)<sup>−1</sup></span> and <span class="texhtml">κ<sub>1</sub> = μ</span>. The limiting case <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i><sup>−1</sup> = 0</span> is a Poisson distribution.</li> 
    <li>The <a href="/wiki/Negative_binomial_distribution" title="Negative binomial distribution">negative binomial distributions</a>, (number of failures before <i>n</i> successes with probability <i>p</i> of success on each trial). The special case <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i> = 1</span> is a geometric distribution. Every cumulant is just <i>n</i> times the corresponding cumulant of the corresponding geometric distribution. The derivative of the cumulant generating function is <i>K</i>&nbsp;'(<i>t</i>)&nbsp;=&nbsp;<i>n</i>·((1&nbsp;−&nbsp;<i>p</i>)<sup>−1</sup>·e<sup>−<i>t</i></sup>−1)<sup>−1</sup>. The first cumulants are κ<sub>1</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;<i>K</i>&nbsp;'(0)&nbsp;=&nbsp;<i>n</i>·(<i>p</i><sup>−1</sup>−1), and <i>κ</i><sub>2</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;<i>K</i>&nbsp;'&nbsp;'(0)&nbsp;=&nbsp;κ<sub>1</sub>·<i>p</i><sup>−1</sup>. Substituting <i>p</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;(μ·<i>n</i><sup>−1</sup>+1)<sup>−1</sup> gives <span class="texhtml"><i>K′</i>(<i>t</i>) = ((<i>μ</i><sup>−1</sup> + <i>n</i><sup>−1</sup>)<i>e</i><sup>−<i>t</i></sup> − <i>n</i><sup>−1</sup>)<sup>−1</sup></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub>1</sub> = <i>μ</i></span>. Comparing these formulas to those of the binomial distributions explains the name 'negative binomial distribution'. The <a href="/wiki/Limiting_case_(mathematics)" title="Limiting case (mathematics)">limiting case</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i><sup>−1</sup> = 0</span> is a Poisson distribution.</li>
   </ul> 
   <p>Introducing the <a href="/wiki/Variance-to-mean_ratio" class="mw-redirect" title="Variance-to-mean ratio">variance-to-mean ratio</a> </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

   <p>the above probability distributions get a unified formula for the derivative of the cumulant generating function:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources.    <p>The second derivative is </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
       <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle K''(t)=g'(t)\cdot (1+e^{t}\cdot (\varepsilon ^{-1}-1))^{-1}}"> 
        <semantics> 
         <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
          <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0"> 
           <msup> 
            <mi>
             K
            </mi> 
            <mo>
             ″
            </mo> 
           </msup> 
           <mo stretchy="false">
            (
           </mo> 
           <mi>

           <mo stretchy="false">
            )
           </mo> 
           <mo>
            =
           </mo> 
           <msup>

         <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">

         </annotation> 
        </semantics> 
       </math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0683532151e33e5827312a4923ba3da5d37c7940" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:36.071ex; height:3.176ex;" alt="K''(t)=g'(t)\cdot (1+e^{t}\cdot (\varepsilon ^{-1}-1))^{-1}"></span>
    </dd>
   </dl> 
   <p>confirming that the first cumulant is <span class="texhtml">κ<sub>1</sub> = <i>K′</i>(0) = <i>μ</i></span> and the second cumulant is <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub>2</sub> = <i>K′′</i>(0) = <i>με</i></span>. The constant random variables <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i> = <i>μ</i></span> have <span class="texhtml"><i>ε</i> = 0</span>. The binomial distributions have <span class="texhtml"><i>ε</i> = 1 − <i>p</i></span> so that <span class="texhtml">0 &lt; <i>ε</i> &lt; 1</span>. The Poisson distributions have <span class="texhtml"><i>ε</i> = 1</span>. The negative binomial distributions have <span class="texhtml"><i>ε</i> = <i>p</i><sup>−1</sup></span> so that <span class="texhtml"><i>ε</i> &gt; 1</span>. Note the analogy to the classification of <a href="/wiki/Conic_sections" class="mw-redirect" title="Conic sections">conic sections</a> by <a href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(mathematics)" title="Eccentricity (mathematics)">eccentricity</a>: circles <span class="texhtml"><i>ε</i> = 0</span>, ellipses <span class="texhtml">0 &lt; <i>ε</i> &lt; 1</span>, parabolas <span class="texhtml"><i>ε</i> = 1</span>, hyperbolas <span class="texhtml"><i>ε</i> &gt; 1</span>. </p> 
   <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulants_of_some_continuous_probability_distributions">Cumulants of some continuous probability distributions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cumulants of some continuous probability distributions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
   <ul>
    <li>For the <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">normal distribution</a> with <a href="/wiki/Expected_value" title="Expected value">expected value</a> μ and <a href="/wiki/Variance" title="Variance">variance</a> σ<sup>2</sup>, the cumulant generating function is <i>K</i>(<i>t</i>) = μ<i>t</i> + σ<sup>2</sup><i>t</i><sup>2</sup>/2. The first and second derivatives of the cumulant generating function are <i>K</i>&nbsp;'(<i>t</i>)&nbsp;=&nbsp;μ&nbsp;+&nbsp;σ<sup>2</sup>·<i>t</i> and <i>K</i>"(<i>t</i>)&nbsp;=&nbsp;σ<sup>2</sup>. The cumulants are κ<sub>1</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;μ, κ<sub>2</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;σ<sup>2</sup>, and κ<sub>3</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;κ<sub>4</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;...&nbsp;=&nbsp;0. The special case σ<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0 is a constant random variable <i>X</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;μ.</li> 
    <li>The cumulants of the <a href="/wiki/Uniform_distribution_(continuous)" title="Uniform distribution (continuous)">uniform distribution</a> on the interval [−1,&nbsp;0] are <i>κ</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> = <i>B</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>/<i>n</i>, where <i>B</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> is the <i>n</i>-th <a href="/wiki/Bernoulli_number" title="Bernoulli number">Bernoulli number</a>.</li> 
    <li>The cumulants of the <a href="/wiki/Exponential_distribution" title="Exponential distribution">exponential distribution</a> with parameter <i>λ</i> are <i>κ</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> = <i>λ</i><sup>−<i>n</i></sup>&nbsp;(<i>n</i>&nbsp;−&nbsp;1)!.</li>
   </ul> 
   <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Some_properties_of_the_cumulant_generating_function">Some properties of the cumulant generating function</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Some properties of the cumulant generating function">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
   <p>The cumulant generating function <i>K</i>(<i>t</i>), if it exists, is <a href="/wiki/Infinitely_differentiable" class="mw-redirect" title="Infinitely differentiable">infinitely differentiable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Convex_function" title="Convex function">convex</a>, and passes through the origin. Its first derivative ranges monotonically in the open interval from the <a href="/wiki/Infimum" class="mw-redirect" title="Infimum">infimum</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Supremum" class="mw-redirect" title="Supremum">supremum</a> of the support of the probability distribution, and its second derivative is strictly positive everywhere it is defined, except for the <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_distribution" title="Degenerate distribution">degenerate distribution</a> of a single point mass. The cumulant-generating function exists if and only if the tails of the distribution are majorized by an <a href="/wiki/Exponential_decay" title="Exponential decay">exponential decay</a>, that is, (<i>see <a href="/wiki/Big_O_notation" title="Big O notation">Big O notation</a></i>,) </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

   <p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
      <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle F}">

The cumulant-generating function will have <a href="/wiki/Vertical_asymptote" class="mw-redirect" title="Vertical asymptote">vertical asymptote</a>(s) at the <a href="/wiki/Infimum" class="mw-redirect" title="Infimum">infimum</a> of such <i>c</i>, if such an infimum exists, and at the <a href="/wiki/Supremum" class="mw-redirect" title="Supremum">supremum</a> of such <i>d</i>, if such a supremum exists, otherwise it will be defined for all real numbers. </p>
   <p>If the <a href="/wiki/Support_(mathematics)" title="Support (mathematics)">support</a> of a random variable <i>X</i> has finite upper or lower bounds, then its cumulant-generating function <i>y</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;<i>K</i>(<i>t</i>), if it exists, approaches <a href="/wiki/Asymptote" title="Asymptote">asymptote</a>(s) whose slope is equal to the supremum and/or infimum of the support, </p>

   <p>yield the <a href="/wiki/Y-intercept" title="Y-intercept"><i>y</i>-intercepts</a> of these asymptotes, since&nbsp;<i>K</i>(0)&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.) </p>
   <p>For a shift of the distribution by <i>c</i>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

   <p>If <i>K</i>(<i>t</i>) is finite for a range <i>t</i><sub>1</sub>&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;Re(<i>t</i>)&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;<i>t</i><sub>2</sub> then if <i>t</i><sub>1</sub>&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;0&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;<i>t</i><sub>2</sub> then <i>K</i>(<i>t</i>) is analytic and infinitely differentiable for <i>t</i><sub>1</sub>&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;Re(<i>t</i>)&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;<i>t</i><sub>2</sub>. Moreover for <i>t</i> real and <i>t</i><sub>1</sub>&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;<i>t</i>&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;<i>t</i><sub>2</sub> <i>K</i>(<i>t</i>) is strictly convex, and <i>K</i>'(<i>t</i>) is strictly increasing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
   <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Some_properties_of_cumulants">Some properties of cumulants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Some properties of cumulants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invariance_and_equivariance">Invariance and equivariance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Invariance and equivariance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <p>The first cumulant is shift-<a href="/wiki/Equivariant" class="mw-redirect" title="Equivariant">equivariant</a>; all of the others are shift-<a href="/wiki/Invariant_(mathematics)" title="Invariant (mathematics)">invariant</a>. This means that, if we denote by <i>κ</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>(<i>X</i>) the <i>n</i>-th cumulant of the probability distribution of the random variable <i>X</i>, then for any constant <i>c</i>: </p> 
   <ul>
    <li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

   </ul> 
   <p>In other words, shifting a random variable (adding <i>c</i>) shifts the first cumulant (the mean) and doesn't affect any of the others. </p> 
   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homogeneity">Homogeneity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Homogeneity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <p>The <i>n</i>-th cumulant is homogeneous of degree <i>n</i>, i.e. if <i>c</i> is any constant, then </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Additivity">Additivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Additivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <p>If <i>X</i> and <i>Y</i> are <a href="/wiki/Statistical_independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Statistical independence">independent</a> random variables then <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>(<i>X</i>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>Y</i>) =&nbsp;<i>κ</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>(<i>X</i>)&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>κ</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>(<i>Y</i>)</span>. </p> 
   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A_negative_result">A negative result</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: A negative result">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <p>Given the results for the cumulants of the <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">normal distribution</a>, it might be hoped to find families of distributions for which <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub><i>m</i></sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;<i>κ</i><sub><i>m</i>+1</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;⋯&nbsp;=&nbsp;0</span> for some <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i>&nbsp;&gt;&nbsp;3</span>, with the lower-order cumulants (orders 3 to <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i>&nbsp;−&nbsp;1</span>) being non-zero. There are no such distributions.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The underlying result here is that the cumulant generating function cannot be a finite-order polynomial of degree greater than&nbsp;2. </p> 
   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulants_and_moments">Cumulants and moments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cumulants and moments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <p>The <a href="/wiki/Moment_generating_function" class="mw-redirect" title="Moment generating function">moment generating function</a> is given by: </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
       <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle M(t)=1+\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {\mu '_{n}t^{n}}{n!}}=\exp \left(\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {\kappa _{n}t^{n}}{n!}}\right)=\exp(K(t)).}"> 
        <semantics> 
         <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
          <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0"> 
           <mi>
            M
           </mi> 
           <mo stretchy="false">
            (
           </mo> 
           <mi>
            t
           </mi> 
           <mo stretchy="false">
            )
           </mo> 
           <mo>
            =
           </mo> 
           <mn>
            1
           </mn> 
           <mo>
            +
           </mo> 
           <munderover> 
            <mo>
             ∑
             <!-- ∑ -->
            </mo> 
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
             <mi>
              n
             </mi> 
             <mo>
              =

   <p>So the cumulant generating function is the logarithm of the moment generating function </p>

   <p>The first cumulant is the <a href="/wiki/Expected_value" title="Expected value">expected value</a>; the second and third cumulants are respectively the second and third <a href="/wiki/Central_moment" title="Central moment">central moments</a> (the second central moment is the <a href="/wiki/Variance" title="Variance">variance</a>); but the higher cumulants are neither moments nor central moments, but rather more complicated polynomial functions of the moments. </p>

   <p>The <i>n</i>-th <a href="/wiki/Moment_(mathematics)" title="Moment (mathematics)">moment</a> <i>μ</i>′<sub><i>n</i></sub> is an <i>n</i>th-degree polynomial in the first <i>n</i> cumulants. The first few expressions are: </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
       <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\mu '_{1}={}&amp;\kappa _{1}\\[5pt]\mu '_{2}={}&amp;\kappa _{2}+\kappa _{1}^{2}\\[5pt]\mu '_{3}={}&amp;\kappa _{3}+3\kappa _{2}\kappa _{1}+\kappa _{1}^{3}\\[5pt]\mu '_{4}={}&amp;\kappa _{4}+4\kappa _{3}\kappa _{1}+3\kappa _{2}^{2}+6\kappa _{2}\kappa _{1}^{2}+\kappa _{1}^{4}\\[5pt]\mu '_{5}={}&amp;\kappa _{5}+5\kappa _{4}\kappa _{1}+10\kappa _{3}\kappa _{2}+10\kappa _{3}\kappa _{1}^{2}+15\kappa _{2}^{2}\kappa _{1}+10\kappa _{2}\kappa _{1}^{3}+\kappa _{1}^{5}\\[5pt]\mu '_{6}={}&amp;\kappa _{6}+6\kappa _{5}\kappa _{1}+15\kappa _{4}\kappa _{2}+15\kappa _{4}\kappa _{1}^{2}+10\kappa _{3}^{2}+60\kappa _{3}\kappa _{2}\kappa _{1}+20\kappa _{3}\kappa _{1}^{3}\\&amp;{}+15\kappa _{2}^{3}+45\kappa _{2}^{2}\kappa _{1}^{2}+15\kappa _{2}\kappa _{1}^{4}+\kappa _{1}^{6}.\end{aligned}}}">

          <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

            <mtable columnalign="right left right left right left right left right left right left" rowspacing="0.8em 0.8em 0.8em 0.8em 0.8em 0.3em 0.3em" columnspacing="0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em" displaystyle="true"> 
             <mtr> 
              <mtd>

                 <!-- μ -->
                </mi>

                 <mn>
                  1
                 </mn> 
                </mrow> 
                <mo>
                 ′
                </mo> 
               </msubsup>

               </mo> 
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

                 <!-- κ -->

   <p>The "prime" distinguishes the moments <i>μ</i>′<sub><i>n</i></sub> from the <a href="/wiki/Moment_about_the_mean" class="mw-redirect" title="Moment about the mean">central moments</a> <i>μ</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>. To express the <i>central</i> moments as functions of the cumulants, just drop from these polynomials all terms in which <i>κ</i><sub>1</sub> appears as a factor: </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
       <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\mu _{1}&amp;=0\\[4pt]\mu _{2}&amp;=\kappa _{2}\\[4pt]\mu _{3}&amp;=\kappa _{3}\\[4pt]\mu _{4}&amp;=\kappa _{4}+3\kappa _{2}^{2}\\[4pt]\mu _{5}&amp;=\kappa _{5}+10\kappa _{3}\kappa _{2}\\[4pt]\mu _{6}&amp;=\kappa _{6}+15\kappa _{4}\kappa _{2}+10\kappa _{3}^{2}+15\kappa _{2}^{3}.\end{aligned}}}">

         <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
          {\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\mu _{1}&amp;=0\\[4pt]\mu _{2}&amp;=\kappa _{2}\\[4pt]\mu _{3}&amp;=\kappa _{3}\\[4pt]\mu _{4}&amp;=\kappa _{4}+3\kappa _{2}^{2}\\[4pt]\mu _{5}&amp;=\kappa _{5}+10\kappa _{3}\kappa _{2}\\[4pt]\mu _{6}&amp;=\kappa _{6}+15\kappa _{4}\kappa _{2}+10\kappa _{3}^{2}+15\kappa _{2}^{3}.\end{aligned}}}
         </annotation> 
        </semantics> 
       </math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68158b6d00b076a0916eb6bb7669096847d0b828" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -11.171ex; width:34.415ex; height:23.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\mu _{1}&amp;=0\\[4pt]\mu _{2}&amp;=\kappa _{2}\\[4pt]\mu _{3}&amp;=\kappa _{3}\\[4pt]\mu _{4}&amp;=\kappa _{4}+3\kappa _{2}^{2}\\[4pt]\mu _{5}&amp;=\kappa _{5}+10\kappa _{3}\kappa _{2}\\[4pt]\mu _{6}&amp;=\kappa _{6}+15\kappa _{4}\kappa _{2}+10\kappa _{3}^{2}+15\kappa _{2}^{3}.\end{aligned}}}"></span>
    </dd>
   </dl> 
   <p>Similarly, the <i>n</i>-th cumulant <i>κ</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> is an <i>n</i>-th-degree polynomial in the first <i>n</i> non-central moments. The first few expressions are: </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
       <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\kappa _{1}={}&amp;\mu '_{1}\\[4pt]\kappa _{2}={}&amp;\mu '_{2}-{\mu '_{1}}^{2}\\[4pt]\kappa _{3}={}&amp;\mu '_{3}-3\mu '_{2}\mu '_{1}+2{\mu '_{1}}^{3}\\[4pt]\kappa _{4}={}&amp;\mu '_{4}-4\mu '_{3}\mu '_{1}-3{\mu '_{2}}^{2}+12\mu '_{2}{\mu '_{1}}^{2}-6{\mu '_{1}}^{4}\\[4pt]\kappa _{5}={}&amp;\mu '_{5}-5\mu '_{4}\mu '_{1}-10\mu '_{3}\mu '_{2}+20\mu '_{3}{\mu '_{1}}^{2}+30{\mu '_{2}}^{2}\mu '_{1}-60\mu '_{2}{\mu '_{1}}^{3}+24{\mu '_{1}}^{5}\\[4pt]\kappa _{6}={}&amp;\mu '_{6}-6\mu '_{5}\mu '_{1}-15\mu '_{4}\mu '_{2}+30\mu '_{4}{\mu '_{1}}^{2}-10{\mu '_{3}}^{2}+120\mu '_{3}\mu '_{2}\mu '_{1}\\&amp;{}-120\mu '_{3}{\mu '_{1}}^{3}+30{\mu '_{2}}^{3}-270{\mu '_{2}}^{2}{\mu '_{1}}^{2}+360\mu '_{2}{\mu '_{1}}^{4}-120{\mu '_{1}}^{6}\end{aligned}}}">

   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulants_and_set-partitions">Cumulants and set-partitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cumulants and set-partitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <p>These polynomials have a remarkable <a href="/wiki/Combinatorics" title="Combinatorics">combinatorial</a> interpretation: the coefficients count certain <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_a_set" title="Partition of a set">partitions of sets</a>. A general form of these polynomials is </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
       <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \mu '_{n}=\sum _{\pi \,\in \,\Pi }\prod _{B\,\in \,\pi }\kappa _{|B|}}"> 
        <semantics> 
         <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
          <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0"> 
           <msubsup> 
            <mi>
             μ
             <!-- μ -->
            </mi> 
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
             <mi>
              n
             </mi> 
            </mrow> 
            <mo>
             ′
            </mo> 
           </msubsup> 
           <mo>
            =
           </mo> 
           <munder> 
            <mo>
             ∑
             <!-- ∑ -->
            </mo> 
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
             <mi>
              π
              <!-- π -->
             </mi> 
             <mspace width="thinmathspace" /> 
             <mo>
              ∈
              <!-- ∈ -->
             </mo> 
             <mspace width="thinmathspace" /> 
             <mi mathvariant="normal">
              Π
              <!-- Π -->
             </mi> 
            </mrow> 
           </munder> 
           <munder> 
            <mo>
             ∏
             <!-- ∏ -->
            </mo> 
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
             <mi>
              B
             </mi> 
             <mspace width="thinmathspace" /> 
             <mo>
              ∈
              <!-- ∈ -->
             </mo> 
             <mspace width="thinmathspace" /> 
             <mi>
              π
              <!-- π -->
             </mi> 
            </mrow> 
           </munder> 
           <msub> 
            <mi>
             κ
             <!-- κ -->
            </mi> 
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
              <mo stretchy="false">
               |
              </mo> 
             </mrow> 
             <mi>
              B
             </mi> 
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
              <mo stretchy="false">
               |
              </mo> 
             </mrow> 
            </mrow> 
           </msub> 
          </mstyle> 
         </mrow> 
         <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
          {\displaystyle \mu '_{n}=\sum _{\pi \,\in \,\Pi }\prod _{B\,\in \,\pi }\kappa _{|B|}}
         </annotation> 
        </semantics> 
       </math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8b5ce1536332e33dab79a8c913dabe069d6be742" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:18.331ex; height:5.676ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \mu '_{n}=\sum _{\pi \,\in \,\Pi }\prod _{B\,\in \,\pi }\kappa _{|B|}}"></span>
    </dd>
   </dl> 
   <p>where </p> 
   <ul>
    <li><span class="texhtml">π</span> runs through the list of all partitions of a set of size <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span>;</li> 
    <li>"<span class="texhtml"><i>B</i> ∈ <span class="texhtml">π</span></span>" means <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i></span> is one of the "blocks" into which the set is partitioned; and</li> 
    <li><span class="texhtml">|<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em;"><i>B</i></span>|</span> is the size of the set <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i></span>.</li>
   </ul> 
   <p>Thus each <a href="/wiki/Monomial" title="Monomial">monomial</a> is a constant times a product of cumulants in which the sum of the indices is <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> (e.g., in the term <span class="texhtml"><i>κ</i><sub>3</sub> <i>κ</i><sub>2</sub><sup>2</sup> <i>κ</i><sub>1</sub></span>, the sum of the indices is 3 + 2 + 2 + 1 = 8; this appears in the polynomial that expresses the 8th moment as a function of the first eight cumulants). A partition of the <a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integer</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> corresponds to each term. The <i>coefficient</i> in each term is the number of partitions of a set of <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> members that collapse to that partition of the integer <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> when the members of the set become indistinguishable. </p> 
   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulants_and_combinatorics">Cumulants and combinatorics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cumulants and combinatorics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <p>Further connection between cumulants and combinatorics can be found in the work of <a href="/wiki/Gian-Carlo_Rota" title="Gian-Carlo Rota">Gian-Carlo Rota</a> and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://sites.google.com/site/jackieneoshen/">Jianhong (Jackie) Shen</a>, where links to <a href="/wiki/Invariant_theory" title="Invariant theory">invariant theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Symmetric_function" title="Symmetric function">symmetric functions</a>, and binomial sequences are studied via <a href="/wiki/Umbral_calculus" title="Umbral calculus">umbral calculus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> </p> 
   <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Joint_cumulants">Joint cumulants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Joint cumulants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
   <p>The <b>joint cumulant</b> of several random variables <i>X</i><sub>1</sub>,&nbsp;...,&nbsp;<i>X</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> is defined by a similar cumulant generating function </p>

   <p>A consequence is that </p>

   <p>The joint cumulant of just one random variable is its expected value, and that of two random variables is their <a href="/wiki/Covariance" title="Covariance">covariance</a>. If some of the random variables are independent of all of the others, then any cumulant involving two (or more) independent random variables is zero. If all <i>n</i> random variables are the same, then the joint cumulant is the <i>n</i>-th ordinary cumulant. </p>
   <p>The combinatorial meaning of the expression of moments in terms of cumulants is easier to understand than that of cumulants in terms of moments: </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
       <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \operatorname {E} (X_{1}\cdots X_{n})=\sum _{\pi }\prod _{B\in \pi }\kappa (X_{i}:i\in B).}"> 
        <semantics> 
         <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
          <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0"> 
           <mi mathvariant="normal">
            E
           </mi>

           </mo>

            (

   <p>Another important property of joint cumulants is multilinearity: </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conditional_cumulants_and_the_law_of_total_cumulance">Conditional cumulants and the law of total cumulance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Conditional cumulants and the law of total cumulance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
    Main article: 
    <a href="/wiki/Law_of_total_cumulance" title="Law of total cumulance">law of total cumulance</a>
   </div>

   <p>where </p> 
   <ul>
    <li>the sum is over all <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_a_set" title="Partition of a set">partitions</a>&nbsp;<span class="texhtml">π</span> of the set {&nbsp;1,&nbsp;...,&nbsp;<i>n</i>&nbsp;} of indices, and</li> 
    <li><span class="texhtml">π</span><sub>1</sub>,&nbsp;...,&nbsp;<span class="texhtml">π</span><sub>b</sub> are all of the "blocks" of the partition <span class="texhtml">π</span>; the expression <i>κ</i>(<i>X</i><sub><span class="texhtml">π</span><sub><i>m</i></sub></sub>) indicates that the joint cumulant of the random variables whose indices are in that block of the partition.</li>
   </ul> 
   <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_statistical_physics">Relation to statistical physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Relation to statistical physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
   <p>In <a href="/wiki/Statistical_physics" title="Statistical physics">statistical physics</a> many <a href="/wiki/Extensive_quantities" class="mw-redirect" title="Extensive quantities">extensive quantities</a> – that is quantities that are proportional to the volume or size of a given system – are related to cumulants of random variables. The deep connection is that in a large system an extensive quantity like the energy or number of particles can be thought of as the sum of (say) the energy associated with a number of nearly independent regions. The fact that the cumulants of these nearly independent random variables will (nearly) add make it reasonable that extensive quantities should be expected to be related to cumulants. </p>
   <p>A system in equilibrium with a thermal bath at temperature <i>T</i> can occupy states of energy <i>E</i>. The energy <i>E</i> can be considered a random variable, having the probability density. The <a href="/wiki/Partition_function_(statistical_mechanics)" title="Partition function (statistical mechanics)">partition function</a> of the system is </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
       <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle Z(\beta )=\langle \exp(-\beta E)\rangle ,\,}"> 
        <semantics> 
         <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
          <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0"> 
           <mi>
            Z
           </mi> 
           <mo stretchy="false">
            (
           </mo> 
           <mi>
            β
            <!-- β -->
           </mi> 
           <mo stretchy="false">
            )

   <p>where <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_beta" title="Thermodynamic beta"><i>β</i></a> =&nbsp;1/(<i>kT</i>) and <i>k</i> is <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann%27s_constant" class="mw-redirect" title="Boltzmann's constant">Boltzmann's constant</a> and the notation <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
      <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \langle A\rangle }">

      <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \operatorname {E} [A]}">

   <p>and is clearly very closely related to the cumulant generating function for the energy. The free energy gives access to all of the thermodynamics properties of the system via its first second and higher order derivatives, such as its <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy">internal energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Specific_heat" class="mw-redirect" title="Specific heat">specific heat</a>. Because of the relationship between the free energy and the cumulant generating function, all these quantities are related to cumulants e.g. the energy and specific heat are given by </p>

Other free energy is often also a function of other variables such as the magnetic field or chemical potential <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

   <p>where <i>N</i> is the number of particles and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
      <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \Omega }">

Again the close relationship between the definition of the free energy and the cumulant generating function implies that various derivatives of this free energy can be written in terms of joint cumulants of <i>E</i> and <i>N</i>. </p> 
   <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
   <p>The history of cumulants is discussed by <a href="/wiki/Anders_Hald" title="Anders Hald">Anders Hald</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> </p>
   <p>Cumulants were first introduced by <a href="/wiki/Thorvald_N._Thiele" title="Thorvald N. Thiele">Thorvald N. Thiele</a>, in 1889, who called them <i>semi-invariants</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> They were first called <i>cumulants</i> in a 1932 paper<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Fisher" title="Ronald Fisher">Ronald Fisher</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Wishart_(statistician)" title="John Wishart (statistician)">John Wishart</a>. Fisher was publicly reminded of Thiele's work by Neyman, who also notes previous published citations of Thiele brought to Fisher's attention.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Stigler" title="Stephen Stigler">Stephen Stigler</a> has said<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> that the name <i>cumulant</i> was suggested to Fisher in a letter from <a href="/wiki/Harold_Hotelling" title="Harold Hotelling">Harold Hotelling</a>. In a paper published in 1929,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Fisher had called them <i>cumulative moment functions</i>. The partition function in statistical physics was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Willard_Gibbs" title="Josiah Willard Gibbs">Josiah Willard Gibbs</a> in 1901.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The free energy is often called Gibbs free energy. In <a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">statistical mechanics</a>, cumulants are also known as <a href="/wiki/Ursell_function" title="Ursell function">Ursell functions</a> relating to a publication in 1927.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
   <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulants_in_generalized_settings">Cumulants in generalized settings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Cumulants in generalized settings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_cumulants">Formal cumulants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Formal cumulants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <p>More generally, the cumulants of a sequence { <i>m</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>&nbsp;: <i>n</i> = 1, 2, 3, ... }, not necessarily the moments of any probability distribution, are, by definition, </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
       <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle 1+\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {m_{n}t^{n}}{n!}}=\exp \left(\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {\kappa _{n}t^{n}}{n!}}\right),}"> 
        <semantics> 
         <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

           </mn>

             </mo> 
             <mn>
              1
             </mn> 
            </mrow> 
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
             <mi mathvariant="normal">
              ∞
              <!-- ∞ -->
             </mi> 
            </mrow> 
           </munderover> 
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
            <mfrac>

                </mi> 
               </mrow>

              <msup> 
               <mi>

               </mi>

           </mo> 
           <mi>

           </mi> 
           <mo>
            ⁡
            <!-- ⁡ -->
           </mo> 
           <mrow> 
            <mo>
             (
            </mo> 
            <mrow> 
             <munderover> 
              <mo>

               <!-- ∑ -->
              </mo> 
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

               </mn> 
              </mrow> 
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                ∞
                <!-- ∞ -->
               </mi> 
              </mrow> 
             </munderover> 
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
              <mfrac> 
               <mrow> 
                <msub> 
                 <mi>
                  κ
                  <!-- κ -->
                 </mi> 
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
                  <mi>
                   n
                  </mi> 
                 </mrow> 
                </msub> 
                <msup> 
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi> 
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
                  <mi>
                   n
                  </mi> 
                 </mrow> 
                </msup> 
               </mrow> 
               <mrow> 
                <mi>
                 n
                </mi> 
                <mo>
                 !
                </mo> 
               </mrow> 
              </mfrac> 
             </mrow> 
            </mrow> 
            <mo>
             )
            </mo> 
           </mrow> 
           <mo>
            ,
           </mo> 
          </mstyle> 
         </mrow> 
         <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
          {\displaystyle 1+\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {m_{n}t^{n}}{n!}}=\exp \left(\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {\kappa _{n}t^{n}}{n!}}\right),}
         </annotation> 
        </semantics> 
       </math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e01bf5100fc20d975f8c46383ff63e79fe13289f" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:34.071ex; height:7.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle 1+\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {m_{n}t^{n}}{n!}}=\exp \left(\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {\kappa _{n}t^{n}}{n!}}\right),}"></span>
    </dd>
   </dl> 
   <p>where the values of κ<sub><i>n</i></sub> for <i>n</i> = 1, 2, 3, ... are found formally, i.e., by algebra alone, in disregard of questions of whether any series converges. All of the difficulties of the "problem of cumulants" are absent when one works formally. The simplest example is that the second cumulant of a probability distribution must always be nonnegative, and is zero only if all of the higher cumulants are zero. Formal cumulants are subject to no such constraints. </p> 
   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bell_numbers">Bell numbers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Bell numbers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <p>In <a href="/wiki/Combinatorics" title="Combinatorics">combinatorics</a>, the <i>n</i>-th <a href="/wiki/Bell_number" title="Bell number">Bell number</a> is the number of partitions of a set of size <i>n</i>. All of the <a href="/wiki/Bell_number#Generating_function" title="Bell number">cumulants of the sequence of Bell numbers are equal to 1</a>. The Bell numbers are the <a href="/wiki/Moment-generating_function#Examples" title="Moment-generating function">moments of the Poisson distribution with expected value 1</a>. </p> 
   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulants_of_a_polynomial_sequence_of_binomial_type">Cumulants of a polynomial sequence of binomial type</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Cumulants of a polynomial sequence of binomial type">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <p>For any sequence { κ<sub><i>n</i></sub>&nbsp;: <i>n</i> = 1, 2, 3, ... } of <a href="/wiki/Scalar_(mathematics)" title="Scalar (mathematics)">scalars</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">field</a> of characteristic zero, being considered formal cumulants, there is a corresponding sequence {&nbsp;μ&nbsp;′&nbsp;: <i>n</i> =&nbsp;1,&nbsp;2,&nbsp;3,&nbsp;...&nbsp;} of formal moments, given by the polynomials above.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="what polynomials (January 2011)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> For those polynomials, construct a <a href="/wiki/Polynomial_sequence" title="Polynomial sequence">polynomial sequence</a> in the following way.          </annotation> 
        </semantics> 
       </math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d4ae3135173574e9c2bcf6cc6a3a04db26c4a934" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.564ex; margin-bottom: -0.274ex; width:78.638ex; height:6.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}p_{6}(x)={}&amp;\kappa _{6}\,x+(6\kappa _{5}\kappa _{1}+15\kappa _{4}\kappa _{2}+10\kappa _{3}^{2})\,x^{2}+(15\kappa _{4}\kappa _{1}^{2}+60\kappa _{3}\kappa _{2}\kappa _{1}+15\kappa _{2}^{3})\,x^{3}\\&amp;{}+(45\kappa _{2}^{2}\kappa _{1}^{2})\,x^{4}+(15\kappa _{2}\kappa _{1}^{4})\,x^{5}+(\kappa _{1}^{6})\,x^{6},\end{aligned}}}"></span>
    </dd>
   </dl> 
   <p>and then generalize the pattern. The pattern is that the numbers of blocks in the aforementioned partitions are the exponents on <i>x</i>. Each coefficient is a polynomial in the cumulants; these are the <a href="/wiki/Bell_polynomials" title="Bell polynomials">Bell polynomials</a>, named after <a href="/wiki/Eric_Temple_Bell" title="Eric Temple Bell">Eric Temple Bell</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
   <p>This sequence of polynomials is of <a href="/wiki/Binomial_type" title="Binomial type">binomial type</a>. In fact, no other sequences of binomial type exist; every polynomial sequence of binomial type is completely determined by its sequence of formal cumulants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
   <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_cumulants">Free cumulants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Free cumulants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
   <p>In the above moment-cumulant formula </p> 
   <dl>
    <dd>
     <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
       <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle E(X_{1}\cdots X_{n})=\sum _{\pi }\prod _{B\,\in \,\pi }\kappa (X_{i}:i\in B)}"> 
        <semantics> 
         <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
          <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0"> 
           <mi>
            E
           </mi> 
           <mo stretchy="false">
            (
           </mo> 
           <msub> 
            <mi>
             X
            </mi> 
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
             <mn>
              1
             </mn> 
            </mrow> 
           </msub> 
           <mo>
            ⋯
            <!-- ⋯ -->
           </mo> 
           <msub> 
            <mi>
             X
            </mi> 
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
             <mi>
              n
             </mi> 
            </mrow> 
           </msub> 
           <mo stretchy="false">
            )
           </mo> 
           <mo>
            =
           </mo> 
           <munder> 
            <mo>
             ∑
             <!-- ∑ -->
            </mo> 
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
             <mi>
              π
              <!-- π -->
             </mi> 
            </mrow> 
           </munder> 
           <munder> 
            <mo>
             ∏
             <!-- ∏ -->
            </mo> 
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
             <mi>
              B
             </mi> 
             <mspace width="thinmathspace" /> 
             <mo>
              ∈
              <!-- ∈ -->
             </mo> 
             <mspace width="thinmathspace" /> 
             <mi>
              π
              <!-- π -->
             </mi> 
            </mrow> 
           </munder> 
           <mi>
            κ
            <!-- κ -->
           </mi> 
           <mo stretchy="false">
            (
           </mo> 
           <msub> 
            <mi>
             X
            </mi> 
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
             <mi>
              i
             </mi> 
            </mrow> 
           </msub> 
           <mo>
            :
           </mo> 
           <mi>
            i
           </mi> 
           <mo>
            ∈
            <!-- ∈ -->
           </mo> 
           <mi>
            B
           </mi> 
           <mo stretchy="false">
            )
           </mo> 
          </mstyle> 
         </mrow> 
         <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
          {\displaystyle E(X_{1}\cdots X_{n})=\sum _{\pi }\prod _{B\,\in \,\pi }\kappa (X_{i}:i\in B)}
         </annotation> 
        </semantics> 
       </math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/be0a24c138c8be4ce64817463e9fa4c5823ded1c" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:37.707ex; height:5.676ex;" alt="{\displaystyle E(X_{1}\cdots X_{n})=\sum _{\pi }\prod _{B\,\in \,\pi }\kappa (X_{i}:i\in B)}"></span>
    </dd>
   </dl> 
   <p>for joint cumulants, one sums over <i>all</i> partitions of the set { 1, ..., <i>n</i> }. If instead, one sums only over the <a href="/wiki/Noncrossing_partition" title="Noncrossing partition">noncrossing partitions</a>, then, by solving these formulae for the <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
      <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \kappa }"> 
       <semantics> 
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"> 
         <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0"> 
          <mi>
           κ
           <!-- κ -->
          </mi> 
         </mstyle> 
        </mrow> 
        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
         {\displaystyle \kappa }
        </annotation> 
       </semantics> 
      </math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/54ddec2e922c5caea4e47d04feef86e782dc8e6d" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.339ex; height:1.676ex;" alt="\kappa "></span> in terms of the moments, one gets <b>free cumulants</b> rather than conventional cumulants treated above. These free cumulants were introduced by Roland Speicher<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and play a central role in <a href="/wiki/Free_probability" title="Free probability">free probability</a> theory.<sup id="cite_ref-Novak-Śniady_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Novak-Śniady-17">[17]</a></sup> In that theory, rather than considering <a href="/wiki/Statistical_independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Statistical independence">independence</a> of <a href="/wiki/Random_variable" title="Random variable">random variables</a>, defined in terms of <a href="/wiki/Tensor_product_of_algebras" title="Tensor product of algebras">tensor products of algebras</a> of random variables, one considers instead <a href="/wiki/Free_independence" title="Free independence">free independence</a> of random variables, defined in terms of <a href="/wiki/Free_product" title="Free product">free products</a> of algebras<sup id="cite_ref-Novak-Śniady_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Novak-Śniady-17">[17]</a></sup>. </p>
   <p>The ordinary cumulants of degree higher than 2 of the <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">normal distribution</a> are zero. The <i>free</i> cumulants of degree higher than 2 of the <a href="/wiki/Wigner_semicircle_distribution" title="Wigner semicircle distribution">Wigner semicircle distribution</a> are zero.<sup id="cite_ref-Novak-Śniady_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Novak-Śniady-17">[17]</a></sup> This is one respect in which the role of the Wigner distribution in free probability theory is analogous to that of the normal distribution in conventional probability theory. </p> 
   <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cumulant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
   <ul>
    <li><a href="/wiki/Entropic_value_at_risk" title="Entropic value at risk">Entropic value at risk</a></li> 
    <li><a href="/wiki/Multiset#Cumulant_generating_function" title="Multiset">Cumulant generating function from a multiset</a></li> 
    <li><a href="/wiki/Cornish%E2%80%93Fisher_expansion" title="Cornish–Fisher expansion">Cornish–Fisher expansion</a></li> 
    <li><a href="/wiki/Edgeworth_expansion" class="mw-redirect" title="Edgeworth expansion">Edgeworth expansion</a></li> 
    <li><a href="/wiki/Polykay" title="Polykay">Polykay</a></li> 
    <li><a href="/wiki/K-statistic" title="K-statistic">k-statistic</a>, a minimum-variance <a href="/wiki/Unbiased_estimator" class="mw-redirect" title="Unbiased estimator">unbiased estimator</a> of a cumulant</li> 
    <li><a href="/wiki/Ursell_function" title="Ursell function">Ursell function</a></li> 
    <li><a href="/wiki/Total_position_spread" title="Total position spread">Total position spread</a> tensor as an application of cumulants to analyse the electronic <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">wave function</a> in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chemistry" title="Quantum chemistry">quantum chemistry</a>.</li>
   </ul>
